KRISHNAMURTI: The whole history of man for forty thousand years or more is part of our consciousness; it is part of our story. We as human beings are the whole history of man. If we grant that, then the only instrument with which we can read this vast complex history is thought. In fact, thought is the only instrument we have. Thought has built the past, and the human mind is the residue, the storehouse, of the entire accumulation of the past: past experience, all the superstitions, all the beliefs, the various Gods, the various rituals, and so on. The whole movement of man-in-time is in the background of every human being. If once we see the fact of this, then we can start from here. Unless we are clear about this, how can we read this immense and complicated book of knowledge?

Jayakar: Obviously human heritage is my heritage. The two are not separate.

Krishnamurti: But very few are willing to accept that.

Jayakar: No, Krishnaji, at one level I am sure most people would accept it.

Krishnamurti: I doubt it.

Jayakar: Human heritage does not belong to a single person. All that has taken place, the ideas that have formed the brain, in fact, the whole development of the human race is common to all of mankind.

Krishnamurti: Wait, Pupul. Most people would not have thought about this. If they have, I doubt whether they would see the fact of this. I want to establish that first. If a few of us see the truth that we carry - all the time - this vast human heritage, then we can proceed. We should see the truth of it, not merely the argument of it or the verbal structure of it.

ACHYUT PATWARDHAN: Sir, would you not concede that although all this may have been accumulated through thought...

Krishnamurti: Time and thought...

A. Patwardhan: Yes, time and thought. You see, sir, when I say that I am the inheritor of the heritage of man, it is not the result of a sequential thought process. When I say that I am the inheritor of it all, it is not as verbalized thought.

Krishnamurti: Do you as a human being, a human being who has studied the history of the world, see the truth that you are the result of the whole of human heritage? Do you see that you are this vast and complex book of the story of man? Do you see it not as a matter of argument but as a fact? Either it is an argumentative concept or it is so - in your blood, in your heart, in your thoughts, in your life.

A. Patwardhan: If it is in my blood, in my whole being, then it is not a sequential thought process; it is a totality.

Krishnamurti: Please, let us not introduce the word ‘totality’. It is so. If we three, at least, see the truth of it, we can proceed from here.

Jayakar: It is as much a truth as the fact that the human body has evolved. Let me put it this way: it is a universal phenomenon. To that extent, I accept that I am human heritage.

Krishnamurti: Right. From there proceed. In me abides the whole story of man: his sorrows, his anxieties, his loneliness, his miseries, his happiness, and so on. This vast story is me. Now, to go back to the question that we raised the other day: What is the instrument with which we read that book?

Jayakar: Before we go to the instrument with which we read the book, what is it that we read?

Krishnamurti: As you are reading it, it is changing. As I am reading it, it is moving.

Jayakar: Yes. As I read, the future comes into existence; the future is projected.

Krishnamurti: Wait a minute. Let us be clear by what we mean by the word ‘future’. The future is the past modifying itself in the present and going on to the future. So the past becomes the future.

Jayakar: The very thought that arises now, contains in it the germ of the future.

Krishnamurti: It contains in it the germ of the future, if there is no alteration.

Jayakar: So we have to be clear about the nature of this ‘what is’ which we have talked about for years.

Krishnamurti: No, you are going into something else, Pupul. Let us begin here. I am the totality of mankind, I am the storehouse of all human endeavour - I don’t want to use that word ‘totality’. I am not aware it. I do not know the nature of it, the content of it, but I want to study it. I want to learn if it is possible to explore the nature of consciousness. I want to explore not my consciousness but the consciousness of man.

Jayakar: The moment you say that I am reading the consciousness of man...

Krishnamurti: Which is the book of man. You see, there is no ‘my’ consciousness.

Jayakar: Yes, of course. The moment you say that I am reading the consciousness of man and not my consciousness, the attitude to that reading has undergone a total change.

Krishnamurti: That’s right. If you are under the illusion that this consciousness is yours - separate from every other consciousness - then we will be moving in two different directions. Unfortunately, most people - including many psychologists - believe that consciousness is separate, that it is individual.

Jayakar: There is a trap there.

Krishnamurti: Yes, there is a trap.

Jayakar: We say that we are the history of mankind, but when we start investigating consciousness...

Krishnamurti: Which is the story of man...

Jayakar: But if we are objectively looking at the history of man...

Krishnamurti: Which is the consciousness of man.

Jayakar: But if we are objectively looking at the history of man, we would read it one way. We would read that history in encyclopaedias. But the moment we see that the history arises within us, within our consciousness, our response will, immediately, be of a totally different nature.

Krishnamurti: That’s what I was coming to. Naturally, if one actually sees - after logical examination and investigation - that one’s consciousness is universal, if one can see that the consciousness that exists within the individual is the consciousness of all human beings, then one’s whole activity of perception changes. Right? Now, do I regard this consciousness as ‘mine’? We must be clear on this point. Do I regard this consciousness as my private ground? You see, it is in the discovery that all human beings are lonely and that all people suffer that I discover something tremendous. To discover the consciousness of the whole of mankind is a tremendous perception.

Jayakar: I would say that the consciousness of mankind is revealed on my private ground.

Krishnamurti: Just a minute. You are saying that by understanding ‘my’ consciousness I recognize that it is the consciousness of man. I go along with that, but at no point must I insist that it is mine.

Jayakar: I cannot say that what is revealed to me in my consciousness is unique to me. It is part of the total consciousness of man, but it is revealed on my ground and, therefore, my relationship to it is very different.

Krishnamurti: Are you saying that in the investigation into my consciousness there is the discovery that it is not my private ground? - That in the investigation into what I have called ‘my’ consciousness as separate from everyone else’s, there is the discovery that what I have called my consciousness is nothing but the consciousness of the rest of man.

Jayakar: But this is not quite so. You see, the observing of that which arises has no place in it for this other state, namely, that it is the consciousness of mankind.

Krishnamurti: I don’t quite follow.

Jayakar: Let us take, for example, an instance of observing loneliness or sorrow arise. This observation - namely, the arising of loneliness in my consciousness - does not bring to the forefront the factor that it is the loneliness of all mankind. At that point, it is just loneliness.

Krishnamurti: It is in the investigation of my loneliness or my sorrow, which I have so far been scrupulously confining to my courtyard and my private ground, that I discover this fact. It is in the discovery that all men are lonely and that all men suffer, that I discover something tremendous. The discovery that it is the whole of mankind that suffers is an enormous perception.

Jayakar: What brings this perception about? Let us see it as though through a microscope. Loneliness arises, or sorrow arises. Then there is an observing of that thing which we call sorrow. What brings in the other element, namely, that I am observing not my petty sorrow, but the sorrow of mankind?

Krishnamurti: No, I don’t observe. Look, Pupulji. I have seen - as must you all have - that wherever you go, loneliness and sorrow live together. This is so in Europe, in America, in India. This factor is shared by all of us. To realize or to admit to oneself - even as a logical conclusion - that this thing is shared by all of us, is a great beginning. You see, change has already taken place.

Jayakar: Yes. But I would like to go back. What is it that has to be observed?

Krishnamurti: I observe sorrow. Loneliness and sorrow are synonymous.

Jayakar: Which are emotional responses to a situation. I feel suddenly a sense of shrinking...

Krishnamurti: A feeling of great loss.

Jayakar: And I look.

Krishnamurti: No, no, no; you don’t look. Let’s be clear. Suppose you have lost a great friend or a wife whom you really loved. What has actually taken place here? There is the ending of that person, and with that ending there is the ending of your entire relationship with that person. And suddenly there is the realization of how utterly lonely you are, because that has been the only relationship that meant something. So when, suddenly, that is gone, there is a sense of great loss.

Now, just hold it for a minute. Remain with it; don’t let thought or any other feeling interfere with that state. If you don’t escape from it, suppress it or analyse it, you will have suddenly discovered this extraordinary phenomenon, namely, that with the loss of some person or some conclusion that you have held most dear, you are shattered and that a certain state of mind has come to an end. Right? You can invent a future, but that future will remain an invention. The ending here is an ending without any future. Can the mind remain with this fact? Can it remain with this fact not as an observer observing the fact? For the observer is the fact. The observer is that state and, therefore, there is no difference between the observer and the thing that he is observing. He is the suffering; he is that ending. It is like a jewel you are holding. The moment you want to part with it, you have entered a different state of consciousness altogether.

Jayakar: I understand.

Krishnamurti: Now, the history of mankind is my history. Right?

Jayakar: Yes.

Krishnamurti: I want to read this book. It must be an extraordinary book. It has not been written. There are no chapters. There are no paragraphs. It is just one tremendous movement.

Jayakar: Can any mind contain the enormity of it?

Krishnamurti: We must begin here. What is the mind and what is the brain? Can I go on with this? Question me as we go along. The brain has infinite capacity. Look what it has done in the technological world - something incredible! But psychologically it has been conditioned through evolution - evolution being time.

Jayakar: Through the concept of time.

Krishnamurti: No. Please go slowly into this; be careful. See that the brain has extraordinary capacity in the technological world and that in the other direction, namely in the psychological world, it has not moved at all. Because it has not moved, it has not flowered. It is conditioned; it is limited. But the mind is not limited.

Jayakar: When you speak of the mind, what is it you speak of?

Krishnamurti: The mind of the universe, the mind of nature; everything that has been created and is in the process of creation is the movement of the mind. And therefore there is no limit to creation.

A. Patwardhan: Are you suggesting that when I say that I am the entire heritage of man, it is not the brain that can take in this factor?

Krishnamurti: It is the brain that takes in this factor, because I have communicated through thought and through words, and you are looking at it through thought, through words. So the communication is verbal and through thought. Sir, please, let us not go back to that...

A. Patwardhan: At present, whatever I understand, I understand through the brain.

Krishnamurti: No, no.

Jayakar: We have moved to another dimension altogether which, if we understand, is this: The brain has done tremendous things in the field of technology, but in the realm of the psyche it is still static. We were talking about the reading of the book of mankind, and my question was: Can a single brain contain it? And Krishnaji came to the brain and the mind. He said that the brain being limited and not having moved, can only move...

Krishnamurti: Within its own circuit.

Jayakar: The mind being the very source of creation has no limit and therefore this whole history of man is within it, if I may use that expression. I don’t want to put it that way but...

Krishnamurti: Go ahead, put it that way.

Jayakar: When you talk of the mind, it is all that is created and is in the process of creation.

Krishnamurti: Pupul, let us be very clear and very careful when we speak of creation. Thought has created, in the physical world, not only the churches, the temples and the mosques but also all the things that are in them. Thought has created wars. Thought has created the conflict between man and man. Thought is responsible for all this and, because thought in itself is limited, thought cannot perceive a mind that is immeasurable. But, obviously, thought tries to understand it because that is its function. The function of thought is to reduce everything to its limited, mechanical, fragmentary activity. Right? And we are saying that as long as the brain is conditioned, it can never understand the immensity of the nature of the mind. If you see this, you will also see ‘your’ responsibility - unfortunately, we have to use words - to uncondition the brain, to uncondition the limitation which thought has imposed on it. Yes, I’ve got it. That’s it.

Jayakar: Is the responsibility to uncondition the brain which is conditioned and, so, cannot move out of its grooves, or to end this movement of the brain?

Krishnamurti: Which is the same thing.

Jayakar: No.

Krishnamurti: I don’t quite understand.

Jayakar: Is what it finds itself unable to do the deconditioning of the brain?

Krishnamurti: No.

Jayakar: Or is it to hold the brain in abeyance so that the perception which is the mind can operate?

Krishnamurti: You are putting in modern language what the old tradition says, namely, that there is, in me, God. There is in me God or some element which is not contaminated, and which then operates on that...

Jayakar: You have drawn the distinction between the brain and the mind. You have drawn the distinction between the conditioned and the non-conditioned state. You have drawn the distinction between that which has an ending...

Krishnamurti: I have not talked about an ending.

Jayakar: Everything that is in the brain as it functions is fragmented.

Krishnamurti: Yes. I have said that we must differentiate between the two meanings of the words. I say that the brain which is limited cannot understand what the mind is. It can only apprehend, grow aware of it when there is no conditioning.

Jayakar: But you went further.

Krishnamurti: That I shouldn’t have spoken. (Laughs)

Jayakar: But you have spoken.

Krishnamurti: That was unfortunate. Dr Bohm and a few others discussed the same matter. You see Pupul, it is really very interesting. Let’s leave the mind alone for the moment. See what an extraordinary capacity the brain has in the technological, scientific, world. See the surgery and all the biological experiments that are taking place. They have transplanted a man-made heart! The incredible scientific world is the activity of thought. But thought is limited because knowledge is limited. The question is whether it can ever be free from its limitation. It can’t. It can never be free of its limitation because it is born of limitation. I don’t know whether you see this.

Jayakar: May I ask a question? What is the distinction between thought and the brain?

Krishnamurti: Thought is the activity of the brain.

Jayakar: Is there anything in the brain apart from thought?

Krishnamurti: (Laughs) I won’t fall into the trap. You are now going back to the old...

Jayakar: I’m not. If you accept that the brain has tremendous capacity and that it is using only a very small part of it, and also if you could do with the psyche what you have done with technology...

Krishnamurti: Then the universe is open to you. That’s what I am saying. The brain has been able to do such extraordinary things technologically. Now, if the brain can free itself from the limitations of the psyche, it will be incredible what it can do. I say that then the brain is the mind; then it is totally free. Then there is no sense of division. There is a sense of completeness, wholeness... I don’t want to enter into it, you understand, Pupul.

Jayakar: If the brain has had the energy, the drive, the insight to pursue technology, why is it...

Krishnamurti: Why are you not willing to turn the other way?

Jayakar: You see, man is prepared to go into space and die. So it’s not a question of death or disappearance...

Krishnamurti: But there’s a great deal behind that - national pride, praise, fame, being a hero. It’s not as if man is really prepared to die. He has been conditioned, through immense propaganda, to die for his country. Look what man has done. In the name of God, in the name of the country, he has burnt people, tortured people.

Jayakar: What is that element that gives human beings the curiosity to drive in the direction of technology?

Krishnamurti: I think our education is responsible for that. Because every culture has emphasized, except for some few dead cultures, that you have to earn a livelihood, that you have to work, work, work, that you have to study, memorize, repeat, repeat. That is all that they say. This morning I met some of the students here at Rishi Valley. Unfortunately, they are the same. They haven’t thought about anything apart from mathematics, history, geography - memorize, repeat, good marks, good job, and so on. If you were to ask them to move a little away from this, they would be lost. You see, they have not given even a thought to the other.

A. Patwardhan: You see, sir, scientists who go to the impossible question are very few. Only a few of them are willing to move in a new direction. So also, today, there are a few who see the present crisis of the survival of humanity. There are a few people today who have sufficient motivation to say that this is the most intolerable predicament for man and that the brain must be explored.

Jayakar: They are exploring the brain but not the psyche.

Krishnamurti: Sir, they are exploring the brain; its functions. Just look at it. The brain has extraordinary capacity - I don’t like to use the word ‘capacity’ because it is based on experience, and capacity based on experience is not capacity at all. There is a different kind of movement which is not based on experience, knowledge. Sir, the brain has done extraordinary things in the technological world, but psychologically it has not moved an inch after all these thousands and thousands of years.

Now if there is a breakthrough, there will be no division between the mind and the energy of the brain. Do you understand? The energy of the brain has done technological work...

Jayakar: Yes, but it has never been released for this.

Krishnamurti: For the other.

A. Patwardhan: It is the energy of attention.

Krishnamurti: Don’t use the word ‘attention’; just stick to the word ‘energy’, sir. Psychologically my energy is practically nil. And I’m saying that when that limitation has been broken down or broken through, there is a totally different energy. So far it is only channelled through technology, which is merely the activity of thought and, therefore, that energy is limited. The breaking down of the psyche is not the energy of thought. Technology is the energy of thought, and the energy of thought is limited.

A. Patwardhan: Agreed.

Krishnamurti: No, no.

Jayakar: We have to probe some more into the instruments that man has. Let us examine those instruments. One is thought, and the others are the senses.

Krishnamurti: Sensory responses; the sensitivity of the senses.

Jayakar: Yes.

Krishnamurti: Hold, hold, hold - the sensitivity of the senses and thought which are both the same.

A. Patwardhan: How, sir?

Jayakar: This is what we must discuss. The sensitivity of the senses and thought - are they both the same?

Krishnamurti: I will show it to you. I have just caught something, but don’t just accept what I say. (Laughs) The sensitivity of the senses. Our senses are controlled by thought - right?

Jayakar: No.

Krishnamurti: Just a minute; don’t take a stand. Wait; I may drop it. If you take a stand, we are lost.

Jayakar: No, I’m not going to take a stand.

Krishnamurti: My senses are now shaped, are controlled by thought. Take, for example, my sense of taste - anything that is bitter, I don’t like, and anything that is sweet, I do. So thought has come in. The question is whether there is a movement of all the senses, total senses, without the interference of thought. Look at the question first.

Have you ever looked at the vast movement of the sea, at the movement of the tides, and at the enormous power of the waves, with all your senses operating? If you do that, there is no interference of thought. Now when thought interferes with the senses, it must inevitably be to limit them or control them. I am going to stick to this.

Jayakar: What you have said is so. There is a challenge and my senses respond according to the conditioning of thought. But there is a response of the senses...

Krishnamurti: Always partial - because thought is always watching, always controlling the senses. I must, I must not.

Jayakar: At some instant there can be water reflected in water.

Krishnamurti: What is this?

Jayakar: It can be like water being reflected in water. There can be a state where there is nothing contained in those senses.

Krishnamurti: Right.

Jayakar: So, I want to push further - if you don’t jump down my throat.

Krishnamurti: (Laughs) I won’t.

Jayakar: That is, there is some connection between the senses - I am not very clear about it. You see, sir, when you think of your brain, you think of it as there (Touches head) somewhere in the head. But when the senses do not operate from thought, do not contain thought, the place of operation changes.

Krishnamurti: That’s right. That’s all I am saying. It’s simple enough. When the senses are observing completely, there is no centre. When you look completely at the movement of the sea, or at the extraordinary sights of the Himalayas when there is not a cloud in the sky, there is no centre; there is no thought. The moment thought comes in, there is a centre. Right?

Jayakar: We have discussed thought, we have discussed the senses. Is there a third movement?

Krishnamurti: Yes. That is the whole point.

A. Patwardhan: What is that? Would you please repeat it?

Jayakar: We have talked of thought, we have talked of the senses. I am asking whether there is any other movement.

Krishnamurti: Ah! This is difficult. Is there an instrument - no, not an instrument but a movement, an action, a state that is not static, but which is yet not a movement of thought? That is your question, isn’t it?

Jayakar: Not a movement of thought, not a movement of the senses...

Krishnamurti: Wait, let’s look at those two things carefully - ‘not thought’ and ‘not the movement of the senses’. When you observe the sea with all your senses, there is no sensory movement. Right? Of course. The senses are not aware that they are heightened. I wonder whether I have made myself clear. Anything that is excellent is not aware of its own excellence. Goodness in the highest sense of the word has no sense of being good.

Jayakar: You are talking of the essence of all thought, of the essence of all the senses. Then it is essence itself that is the instrument.

Krishnamurti: No; leave it for the moment. When there is a heightened excellence of the senses, the senses are not aware. We only realize that the senses are fully awake when thought comes in. The heightened state has already gone.

Now when thought is aware of its own tremendous limitation, then it is broken through. But to realize that, is not to verbalize it; it is to actually see that thought has no place in the movement... Wait a minute, I must go into this carefully. Pupul, what are we trying to get at?

Jayakar: We are reading the story of mankind and we are asking what the instrument with which we probe is.

Krishnamurti: I will tell you. The story of mankind is an endless movement. It has no beginning and no end. If you once grant that it has no ending... But my brain being limited, is looking for an ending. Right? So I am approaching the book to find out what the end of all this is.

Jayakar: The search is for the end.

Krishnamurti: Of course, of course. But to realize that there is no end - do you realize what it means?

To realize that there is no end is to enter into something called love. Love has no end. I may love my wife - she dies or I die, but the thing called love goes on; it has no end. But as I have identified myself with my wife, I either say that my love has gone or I begin to love someone else. And all that is no more than pleasure. I don’t want to go into it now.

So, how do you read the book? You don’t read it at all. Right? There is no book to read. And when you come to this really deep point, namely, that this book has no end and no beginning, you realize that you are the book. This does not mean that you become eternal, but that life as this movement has no end. It is then the universe. It is then the cosmos. It is then the whole thing.

Do you think that I have been talking nonsense? If someone heard all this - someone who was serious, of course - would it all sound so extraordinarily wild? But what I am saying is not wild. It is logical and very clear and can be stated in Sorbonne, Harvard or in Delhi. It will hold water.

Rishi Valley, 19 December 1982

11. Uncovering the Source

Krishnamurti: Can we have a dialogue on something that not only appeals to the Western mind but also to the Indian mind that has, perhaps, thought about these things much longer than the Western world? Considering both the West and the East, what do you think would be of the greatest significance? What do you think would be something that is enduring, something that is worthwhile?

PUPUL JAYAKAR: Sir, most of our lives are so futile.

Krishnamurti: Yes.

Jayakar: And unless one discovers within oneself the capacity to leap out of the futility, one will never be able to have a creative spring. You see, sir, when the mind has a creative spring, whatever be the circumstances, one seems to go beyond them. And that happens when the mind is not dependent on anything, and when it has some space, some perception.

I have been wondering for the last few months - what is the ground of the creative?

Krishnamurti: I wonder what you mean by ‘creative’? An artist says he is creative. But would you call the activity of a poet, of a thinker, or even of a scientist who makes a new discovery creative activity?

Jayakar: Perhaps.

Krishnamurti: But it is limited.

Jayakar: Sir, why do you bring in the word ‘limited’?

Krishnamurti: All right; let us not use the word ‘limited’. Instead let’s use the word partial.

Jayakar: But, sir, I can’t bring in the word ‘partial’, because I don’t know the other.

Krishnamurti: It’s partial because it is not related to daily life. A scientist may discover extraordinary things and, so, call his life creative. But the greatest scientist may lead a very mediocre life.

Jayakar: But you see, that’s why I did not speak of a creative action...

Krishnamurti: But of a creative mind?

Jayakar: But of a ground, a mind, a perception, which rests in the creative.

Krishnamurti: I think you should make the question a little more clear.

Jayakar: You have never answered any questions on the ground of manifestation. Let’s take it at the simplest level. This ‘coming to be’ of anything.

Krishnamurti: Of birth - that of a baby or a mango tree or a bird? Are you asking what the source of all life - both the manifest and the non-manifest - is?

Jayakar: Yes. If it is possible, I would like to probe into what you have said just now: the manifest, the unmanifest and the pre-manifest... I won’t even use the word unmanifest.

Krishnamurti: Are we discussing this subject in a technological, scientific sense or are we probing into something which you and I don’t know? Just a minute. I want to make it clear. Because, after all, we know about the birth of a baby. We know how it comes into being.

Jayakar: One may know how it comes into being, but one still does not know...

Krishnamurti: What?

Jayakar: The quality of life which pervades it. You see, sir, the actuality of birth is very different from the description of birth.

Krishnamurti: Yes.

Jayakar: It is the same with everything.

Krishnamurti: The description is not the thing. The explanation is not the actual.

Jayakar: But you cannot live through life without going into this ‘coming into existence’.

Krishnamurti: I don’t quite follow what you are trying to convey. I am not simply putting obstructions; I really don’t quite follow. Can we talk about what the origin, the beginning, of all life or of all existence is, without having to move further and further back infinitely but by trying to discover or come upon something which is the beginning of all things? Do you follow what I mean? You see, various religious people say, ‘God is the origin of everything’. But ‘God’ is just a word, and a word doesn’t convey... Do you follow what I am saying?

Jayakar: Yes.

Krishnamurti: We are concerned right now about the origin of all life. Are we delving really deeply - without any belief, without any dogma, and so on - or are we just having a theoretical dialogue, where we are moving between the actual and the non-actual, and trying to probe into something with thought? I don’t know if you understand what I am saying.

Jayakar: I understand what you are saying. You see, sir, we have narrowed the word ‘creative’ to mean, as you said, painting, or writing a book, or discovering something in science.

Krishnamurti: That’s right.

Jayakar: But basically, the whole meaning of a tree, a human being, the earth, the sky...

Krishnamurti: Man has asked this question...

Jayakar: Of course he’s asked this question.

Krishnamurti: He has asked what the meaning and the origin of all this is.

Jayakar: Yes. Where does it arise?

Krishnamurti: That’s it. That’s what you are asking, isn’t it? What is the ground from which all this arises?

Jayakar: Yes.

Krishnamurti: What is the source of all existence, all life, all action? Now, how does one inquire into that? What is our approach to it? How do we come to investigate into something that demands an extraordinary freedom (and the very word freedom means love) and an absolutely non-conditioned mind, if I can use that word? It requires that quality of mind which is both practical and sensitive and which has the quality of great compassion.

Jayakar: I can’t start with that.

Krishnamurti: No. So how do we come to that point and move on from there?

Jayakar: Sir, if you put it that way, I am stuck; I can’t move.

Krishnamurti: No, I am just asking; I don’t say it must be that. Isn’t that the process of inquiry?

Jayakar: I say that this question arises in my mind and I would like to move with this question.

Krishnamurti: Right.

Jayakar: If I say that the mind can question only when it is free and, therefore, love, what do I do?

Krishnamurti: You can’t do anything. But how do you inquire into something, a question, that man has asked for millions of years? How do you inquire into something that he gave a name to and then was satisfied with? We are not doing that. We are asking, how does a mind inquire into something that must be extraordinary, that must have a quality of not only the universal, the cosmic - if one can use that word - but something that is of supreme order? How does one’s inquiry begin? Where does it begin? If you inquire with thought, that doesn’t lead very far.

Jayakar: No. You asked: How does the inquiry begin?

Krishnamurti: Yes, what is the manner, what is the approach of a mind that wants to inquire into something that it doesn’t know and which demands an extraordinary quality of subtlety, of order, and so on? Where do I begin?

Jayakar: Obviously by being aware of the disorder within oneself.

Krishnamurti: You see, Pupul, I am after all the manifest. I have been born. I am a human being.

Jayakar: Yes, but obviously, sir, there can be no other starting point.

Krishnamurti: The world outside, the world inside. What is the criterion which measures the outer and the inner? What is the measurement? I am not using the word ‘judgement’; I am using, purposely, the word ‘measurement’.

Jayakar: But is it necessary to measure?

Krishnamurti: If I inquire into myself in a monastery, I can deceive myself enormously. But if I have a measure - just let me use that word for the moment - of what actually is happening in the world outside of me, if I can observe all that without any bias, and if I can see and relate what is happening outside to what is happening inside, then I will see that it is one movement and not two separate movements.

Jayakar: Sir, I am not in a monastery. I am in the midst of life.

Krishnamurti: That’s right.

Jayakar: And being in the midst of life, I see action at various levels - action that is both connected with me and disconnected with me. I also see the responses within me to actions. I see the capacity which I may have gathered over the years. I have even been able to remain without reacting to it. I see all that. And I move into that. I move with it.

Krishnamurti: You are it. Don’t say, ‘I move with it’.

Jayakar: Yes, I am it.

Krishnamurti: You are that.

Jayakar: But you see it is easier to say, ‘I am it’ with regard to the interior movement. To see that with regard to an exterior movement is much more difficult. If you tell me that I am all the wars which are taking place in the world, that’s very difficult for me to see.

Krishnamurti: No. Pupul, we are responsible - in the deepest sense of that word - for all the wars that are taking place.

Jayakar: Yes, but that’s a distant thing to me. You must understand that that responsibility is a distant responsibility. Perhaps I might say ‘Yes, I am responsible’, if I take ‘responsibility’ to the very end, to the ultimate meaning of it all, but I can’t link it in the same way to what is within me.

Krishnamurti: Quite.

Jayakar: Naturally, a response within me is a living response.

Krishnamurti: I hope my next question won’t deviate from what we are discussing. You see, I want to know why you don’t feel total responsibility for the wars, the brutality, the terrible things that are happening in the world? Why don’t you feel totally responsible?

Jayakar: How is one totally responsible? By being born?

Krishnamurti: No; but my entire way of living, my entire way of thinking and of acting - as a nationalist, this or that - has contributed to the present state of the world.

Jayakar: You are making it so difficult. A man commits a sadistic murder. I can’t say that I am responsible for that sadistic murder.

Krishnamurti: Of course not.

Jayakar: Sir, you know, when you take it to that extent, it is impossible for me to feel the reality of it.

Krishnamurti: Let’s leave that for a moment. I have asked the question. Leave it.

Jayakar: Yes, I think it’s better to leave that. But let’s probe into the ground of existence which is the ‘isness’ of life.

Krishnamurti: ‘Isness’ - the verb ‘is’.

Jayakar: The only way to probe is to move into oneself, whatever that means.

Krishnamurti: All right. Let’s take for the moment those words - to go, to move or to enter into the whole complex of oneself. Now, I can’t enter into it as an observer from the outside, for I am all that.

Jayakar: Yes, it’s not even that I state what I am.

Krishnamurti: Yes.

Jayakar: I don’t state; I discover, I uncover.

Krishnamurti: Uncover, yes. ‘Uncover’ rather than ‘discover’.

Jayakar: I uncover what I am. And in uncovering what I am, I comprehend that one is uncovering the whole existence of man.

Krishnamurti: Yes.

Jayakar: That’s possible to see.

Krishnamurti: Yes, that’s very simple.

Jayakar: So in this journey of uncovering, the superficial things are swept clean.

Krishnamurti: That’s fairly simple.

Jayakar: But once the superficial is over, the room has been swept.

Krishnamurti: Isn’t that important too? Who sweeps the room? What does it mean - to have swept the room? Do you follow what I am asking? Is the sweeping, or cleansing, or uncovering, a complete moving away from all the superficial reactions, superficial conditionings, and a trying to enter into the nature of the movement that conditions the mind?

Jayakar: Obviously, sir, you can’t say that you have swept the room and it is over...

Krishnamurti: Yes.

Jayakar: The dust gathers again.

Krishnamurti: Yes.

Jayakar: Sweeping is a movement which is part of living. The grosser elements can certainly be eliminated. You see, sir, it is possible to sweep away the more obvious things, but the subtler things survive in corners which you have not been able to get to.

Krishnamurti: Yes, that’s right. But, let’s go a little more into the obvious things...

Jayakar: You know for instance, Krishnaji, ambition...

Krishnamurti: Yes.

Jayakar: Or envy...

Krishnamurti: Yes, and hatred. But you know, Pupul, can one really be free of hatred? Can one really wipe it away? Let’s go into it a little bit. To be free of hatred must mean something extraordinary. Can one be free of all sense of aggression, all sense of the enemy? There is no enemy. The enemy is you.

Jayakar: But hatred is a different thing.

Krishnamurti: That’s why I want to discuss it.

Jayakar: It is different from the quality of aggression. Let’s go into it a little, sir.

Krishnamurti: Aggression is related to hatred, because it’s part of the same movement. An aggressive nation or an aggressive person inevitably hurts another, and that hurt breeds hatred.

Jayakar: Yes, that’s why I say that there are the grosser things and there are the subtler things. Hatred - anyone who has known hatred - knows that hatred is a very powerful and a very destructive thing. But aggression may, to some extent, be part of one’s nature.

Krishnamurti: Yes, of course, it’s part...

Jayakar: It may be that it is part of your make-up as a human being.

Krishnamurti: Yes, to survive, and all the rest.

Jayakar: You may be more assertive than another, that is aggression. To be assertive is not hatred. That’s why I made the distinction between the grosser things which can be swept clean and the...

Krishnamurti: But how does one know what is gross and what is subtle? What is the mind that says that this is... Pupul, move. Let’s move.

Jayakar: That’s why I think the only way to move into this is to see that nothing is trivial.

Krishnamurti: That nothing, that no reaction...

Jayakar: Is trivial.

Krishnamurti: Yes, that nothing is trivial and that all things, all reactions, have their source in one’s conditioning.

Jayakar: You know, sir, I recently saw the casting of a tremendous metal cauldron - a cauldron that was about seven feet in diameter. The slightest flaw - it didn’t matter how slight it was - would have cracked the cauldron. And it’s exactly like that. It doesn’t matter how slight, how subtle it is, it still cracks the investigation.

Krishnamurti: I understand that. Aren’t you saying to me that it needs great training, great discipline, great attention, a sense of tremendous control, energy and very, very subtle hands - like that of the potter who moulds some marvellous thing?

Jayakar: Doesn’t it?

Krishnamurti: Oh yes, it does.

Jayakar: And this is where I think that one takes the word that you use, the word ‘free’, and interprets it to mean a certain flabbiness of the spirit.

Krishnamurti: Oh no, no, no.

Jayakar: Please let us pursue this...

Krishnamurti: Yes.

Jayakar: As it is very important.

Krishnamurti: It’s not flabbiness of spirit. Good Lord!

Jayakar: No, but sir, it may mean for me the non-acceptance of any authority, not just that of authority in the realm of the psyche, in the realm of the spirit. I may take it to mean licence and, therefore, think it unnecessary to do certain things.

Krishnamurti: Oh, no, no, no.

Jayakar: I may think that I can live a futile life, a trivial life. I may think that it just doesn’t matter; I may think that nothing matters.

Krishnamurti: No, no. You see, Pupul, the very word ‘freedom’, as far as I understand, means affection, love...

Jayakar: And a tremendous discipline. Might we use the word ‘discipline’?

Krishnamurti: I know you are using the word discipline, but I am not sure...

Jayakar: I am speaking of ‘discipline’ as a demand for such watchfulness that the trivial does not, at any time, creep in.

Krishnamurti: No, you see, the point is this: Does watchfulness, which is awareness, need training? Does it need discipline? We have to understand the meaning of that word ‘discipline’.

Jayakar: Sir, we commonly mean by discipline some kind of regimentation. For example, if I were to sit cross-legged and look, unblinkingly, at the wall every morning, forcing myself, at the same time, not to have a single thought, that would be considered discipline. But I mean by ‘discipline’ the mind awakening to the fact that it must be aware of every movement within itself. Sir, that also is a discipline.

Krishnamurti: No, Pupul, ‘discipline’ in the general sense is connected with training, with conformity, imitation, restraint.

Jayakar: No, but if there is diligence... Sir, without diligence nothing is possible. So may we discard the word ‘discipline’, and put in the word ‘diligence’?

Krishnamurti: Wait, wait. Go slowly. ‘To be diligent’ means to be aware of what you are doing, to be aware of what you are thinking, to be aware of your reactions.

Jayakar: Yes.

Krishnamurti: And from those reactions, to observe the actions taking place. Now, the question is: In that observation, in that awareness, is the action controlled, is the action put into a certain framework?

Jayakar: No, obviously not.

Krishnamurti: What I am objecting to altogether - and, of course, this is subject to discussion - is the word ‘discipline’.

Jayakar: But, sir, you have become, if I may say so, allergic to that word.

Krishnamurti: No, I am not allergic; I have got an allergy but I am not allergic to that word. (Laughs)

Jayakar: No, sir, you are restricting the use of that word to mean merely the putting of something into a framework.

Krishnamurti: Yes, but I also hold that the very act of learning is its own discipline.

Jayakar: Yes. But how does the act of learning come to be? Can we, please sir, take it one step back? From where does the need for observation arise? Why should I observe?

Krishnamurti: For a very simple reason, namely, to see whether it’s possible for a human mind to change something, to change itself, to change the world which is entering into such a catastrophic area.

Jayakar: Yes, but sir, if I start with that premise...

Krishnamurti: No, no, it is not a premise; it is so.

Jayakar: All right. If I start there, or if I start with sorrow - which is very often the real ground from which one starts...

Krishnamurti: Yes.

Jayakar: The ground is really sorrow. But I think we have moved away.

Krishnamurti: Yes, I was coming to that.

Jayakar: So, let’s go back to the question.

Krishnamurti: Now, what we started out with was an inquiry into the origin, the ground, of all life.

Jayakar: Yes.

Krishnamurti: To inquire into that, you have to inquire into yourself, because you are the expression of that.

Jayakar: Yes.

Krishnamurti: You are life.

Jayakar: Yes.

Krishnamurti: Now we are trying to discuss the origin of that, and I can only do that by understanding myself.

Jayakar: Yes.

Krishnamurti: Now, the ‘myself’ is so terribly complex. The self is a living complex; it is a messy, disordered entity. How do we approach a problem that is complex, a problem that is not to be easily diagnosed? Do we say, ‘This is right’, ‘This is wrong’, ‘It should be’, ‘It should not be’?

Jayakar: But is it not because one starts with an attention which is looking for an ordered entity, that one finds disorder?

Krishnamurti: I am not looking for disorder or order. We are missing something. I said the world is in disorder. I observe it; I see that it is so. I begin with that.

Jayakar: Yes.

Krishnamurti: There is disorder outside and disorder inside. I am in disorder. Let’s leave it there for the moment. Now, how do I comprehend or become aware of the origin of disorder? If I can begin to understand the origin of disorder, I can move more and more deeply into something which may be total chaos but which is total order. Do you follow what I mean?

Jayakar: Isn’t it by being as simple as possible?

Krishnamurti: Yes, that’s what I am trying to say.

Jayakar: And I have certain instruments of inquiry: eyes, ears, the other senses.

Krishnamurti: Yes, but you don’t inquire with your ears or with your eyes.

Jayakar: Don’t you?

Krishnamurti: A little bit, yes. I inquire when I look around at the world outside, or when I read something. The question is: Do I look into myself with my eyes? I can see myself in a mirror with my optic eyes. But I can’t see the complexity of myself with my eyes. I must be aware, sensitively, without any choice, of this condition.

Jayakar: Why do you say, sir, that you cannot be aware with your eyes?

Krishnamurti: What do you mean ‘with your eyes’? Do you mean the inward eye?

Jayakar: No. You see, there is a way of looking out and there is a way of looking in, a way of listening in.

Krishnamurti: You must be a little careful here, because it’s misleading.

Jayakar: Let’s go into it. Is there any other way?

Krishnamurti: Yes, I think there is.

Jayakar: Let’s go into the other way, sir. Is the eye, the ear, not part of the other way?

Krishnamurti: Breathing? Hearing? Seeing? Feeling? Those are actually sensory responses, right? I see that colour. I hear that noise. I taste something, and so on. All those are sensory responses.

Jayakar: Yes. But is there not a seeing of anger - a reaction of anger - a listening to a reaction of anger?

Krishnamurti: Do you listen with your ears or do you observe anger?

Jayakar: How do you observe anger?

Krishnamurti: When you are angry, you look at the cause and the effect of anger.

Jayakar: When you are angry, you can’t...

Krishnamurti: Yes, you can’t at that moment. So, later on...

Jayakar: You see the nature of the mind which has been in a state of anger. But you see, sir, the word you use is ‘see’. You say that you see the nature of the mind...

Krishnamurti: I understand what you are asking. You are asking: The very act of listening, the very act of feeling, inwardly - are those acts performed by the eyes? That is, do we see, do we hear - inwardly - with our eyes, and with our sensory ears?

Jayakar: You see, sir, if you put it that way, then you never get to the point, because the sensory ear is so used to listening to the outer that it can never comprehend what it is to listen to the within.

Krishnamurti: But would it help if we talked about perception?

Jayakar: No, sir; I say it would help if you talked about seeing, about listening with the eye and the ear - because there is a seeing and listening with the eye and the ear.

Krishnamurti: Wait a minute. I hear you make that statement. I’ve understood the words and see the meaning of what you are saying. Right?

Jayakar: Yes.

Krishnamurti: The verbal communication has taken place.

Jayakar: Yes.

Krishnamurti: But the deeper significance...

Jayakar: But that has also taken place, sir. While I am listening to you and seeing you, I am also listening and seeing my own mind, the ground of the mind.

Krishnamurti: No.

Jayakar: Then what is taking place?

Krishnamurti: Who is listening?

Jayakar: There is listening. I am not saying who is listening.

Krishnamurti: But, just a minute, Pupul, we must be clear on this point. Let’s go into it a little more carefully.

Jayakar: Take an act where you are totally attentive. What is the state of the mind in that act of being totally attentive?

Krishnamurti: What is the state of action that’s born out of complete attention? I’ll answer it. But to answer that question, one must first understand what we mean by complete attention. Attention is not concentration. I think that’s clear.

Jayakar: No, sir, it’s not.

Krishnamurti: No, you see, Pupul, it’s not a case for mere agreement. I want to be clear on that.

Jayakar: Of course, sir, attention is not concentration.

Krishnamurti: Attention means that there is no centre from which you are attending.

Jayakar: No, of course not.

Krishnamurti: Don’t just say, ‘Of course not’; please see what’s implied in it.

Jayakar: You see, sir, I would like to ask you one thing. Are we still dusting the periphery?

Krishnamurti: No, no; I don’t want to dust the periphery.

Jayakar: If you are not dusting the periphery, then, when you ask that question, I cannot understand.

Krishnamurti: I want to be clear, that’s all.

Jayakar: Unless I understand what attention is, I can’t even take the first step.

Krishnamurti: No, that’s why I want to be clear. What does attention - to attend completely - mean?

Jayakar: You see, ‘to attend completely’ is for the ‘I’ not to be there.

Krishnamurti: Yes, that is the real thing. When there is attention, there is no ‘I’. It isn’t a state of I am attending, but only a state of mind which is wholly attentive.

Jayakar: With all the senses...

Krishnamurti: With all the senses and the whole body.

Jayakar: The whole being is awake, if I may say so.

Krishnamurti: Yes, you can use that word.

Jayakar: And if you are in that state when your being is awake, then you can listen, you can observe; you can proceed from there.

Krishnamurti: You appear to be going off again. I want to inquire into myself. Right? That’s what we are saying. Because I myself am life and if I am to inquire into what I am, my inquiry has to be correct, accurate, not distorted. It is only then that I may come upon the ground, the beginning of all life. It is only then that the origin may be discovered, may be uncovered.

Jayakar: If we start from there, we will find that the ‘I’ is there in the first step.

Krishnamurti: Yes. The first step is to see clearly, to hear clearly.

Jayakar: But the ‘I’ is there...

Krishnamurti: Yes, of course.

Jayakar: There’s the observer...

Krishnamurti: And the observed. Now, wait a minute, Pupul, do not move away from that. I know that there is the observer and the observed. Now, I am inquiring whether that is actually so. So far I have taken it for granted.

Jayakar: Obviously, sir, when I first start inquiring, I start with the observer.

Krishnamurti: Yes, I start with the observer.

Jayakar: Now you have placed that thought, that doubt, in my mind and I ask, ‘Is there the observer?’

Krishnamurti: Is there an observer separate from the observed?

Jayakar: Having that statement within me, I look for the observer.

Krishnamurti: Yes. Who is the observer? Let’s look into this slowly. Because if I understand the observer, then perhaps the observer may see the falseness of the division between the observer and the observed.

Jayakar: Who will see?

Krishnamurti: The point is not who will see, but the perception of what is true. You see, what is of importance is perception, not who sees.

Jayakar: So, the seeing of what the truth of the observer is will end the state of division.

Krishnamurti: Yes, that is what I have said a thousand times.

Jayakar: Yes, for this instant it is so.

Krishnamurti: Go ahead. What are you trying to say?

Jayakar: What I am saying is that diligence or discipline is needed so that the inquiry is alive within one.

Krishnamurti: That does not need training.

Jayakar: I am not talking of training. I am using the word ‘discipline’ without bringing in the word ‘training’. I say that I cannot expect to have an understanding of what you say unless the mind is awake and is diligent about being awake.

Krishnamurti: Go ahead.

Jayakar: You cannot deny this.

Krishnamurti: No. It has to be diligent; it has to be watchful; it has to be attentive, subtle, hesitant. It has to be all that. I can only inquire into myself through my reactions - the way I think, the way I act, the way I respond to the environment, the way I observe my relationship to another.

Jayakar: I find that as I first observe myself, the responses and reactions are rapid, confused, continuous.

Krishnamurti: I know; they are contradictory, and so on.

Jayakar: In the very observing, some space comes into being.

Krishnamurti: Yes, some space, some order.

Jayakar: That’s just the beginning, sir.

Krishnamurti: I know. Why do we stay ‘at the beginning’? Pupul, I would like to ask a question. Is it necessary to go through all this? Is it necessary to watch my actions, to watch my reactions, my responses? Is it necessary to observe, diligently, my relationship with another? Must I go through all this? Or...?

Jayakar: The fact is, sir, one has gone through all this.

Krishnamurti: You may have gone through it because you have accepted that pattern.

Jayakar: No.

Krishnamurti: Just wait a minute. You see that is what we have all done - the thinkers, the sannyasis, the monks, and...

Jayakar: And Krishnamurti.

Krishnamurti: I’m not sure. Wait, just a minute. I want to discuss this point seriously.

Jayakar: Either you have, in the past thirty years, taken a jump or...

Krishnamurti: Wait a minute. Let’s look at it. We have accepted this pattern of examination, analysis and investigation. We have accepted these reactions, we have paid attention to them. We have watched the ‘self’ and so on. Now, there is something in it which rings a false note - at least to me.

Jayakar: You mean to say that a person caught in the whole confusion of existence...

Krishnamurti: Pupul, he won’t even listen to all this.

Jayakar: There has to be space in order to listen.

Krishnamurti: Yes.

Jayakar: How does that space arise?

Krishnamurti: You suffer. Now, you can either say, ‘I must find out’ or you merely say, ‘God exists, and I am comforted by that’.

Jayakar: No sir. You ask: Is it necessary to go through all this?

Krishnamurti: Yes, that is what I am asking, for I think that it may not be necessary.

Jayakar: Then show me how.

Krishnamurti: Wait, I’ll show it to you. Let’s go into it. We shall call, for the moment, your diligent watching of your reactions, the analytical process of inquiry. Now, this analytical, self-investigative process, this constant watching, man has done for thousands of years.

Jayakar: He has not. He has done something quite different.

Krishnamurti: What has he done that’s different?

Jayakar: He has looked at his mind and tried to suppress...

Krishnamurti: Ah, you see, that’s part of the pattern. Suppress, escape, substitute, transcend - all that is within that framework.

Jayakar: It’s not the same thing as to observe without doing anything about the observation.

Krishnamurti: No, Pupul, if I may point out - perhaps I may be wrong - you are not answering my question: Must I go through all this?

Jayakar: You see the word ‘must’ is a very...

Krishnamurti: All right, I won’t use the word ‘must’, but the question remains. Is it necessary, is it imperative, is it essential, that I go through all this?

Jayakar: No, but are you trying to say that out of the middle of chaos you can leap to a state of total non-chaos?

Krishnamurti: No, I won’t put it that way.

Jayakar: Then what are you saying?

Krishnamurti: No, wait a minute. I am saying, very clearly, that humanity has gone through this process. It has been the pattern of our existence - of course, some have gone through the process more diligently, sacrificing everything, inquiring, analysing, searching, and so on. You do this too, and at the end of it all you may be just a dead entity.

Jayakar: No, it may not be so.

Krishnamurti: May not be. You see, Pupul, very few - very, very, few - have got out of it.

Jayakar: Yes, but that is why I say that it may not be. But, sir, you are saying that it - this whole process - is not necessary.

Krishnamurti: I know. So your point is, if it is not necessary, then show me the other.

Jayakar: Yes, show me the other.

Krishnamurti: I’ll show it to you. But first step out of this.

Jayakar: You see, sir...

Krishnamurti: Wait, wait, wait. I’ll show it to you.

Jayakar: But look what you are asking.

Krishnamurti: I know.

Jayakar: If I step out of the other, it’s already there.

Krishnamurti: Of course. Step out. That’s what I am saying. Don’t take time to go through all this.

Jayakar: No, but what is meant by ‘step out of it’?

Krishnamurti: I’ll tell you what I mean. Let me talk a little. I recognize or I perceive - whatever be the word you use - that man has tried this process of introspective observation, diligence and so on, for a million years in different ways, and somehow the mind is not clear at the end of it. I see this very clearly. I see that somehow this movement is very, very shallow. Now, can you listen to that statement - that the whole process is shallow - and actually see the truth of it? If you do, it means that your disordered mind is now quiet; it is listening to find out. Your confused, traditional mind now not only says that you are accustomed to this diligent observation of all your activities but also that the entire process is really very superficial. Once you see the truth of this, you are out of it. It’s like putting away something utterly meaningless.

Wait; let me put it another way. My mind is disorderly. My life is disorderly. You come along and say, ‘Be diligent; be watchful of your actions, of your thoughts, of your relationship’. You say, ‘Be watchful all the time’. And I say that that’s impossible because my mind won’t allow me to be diligent all the time. It is not diligent; it is negligent, and I struggle between these two.

Jayakar: But do you mean to say, Krishnaji, that a mind which is not capable of observing...

Krishnamurti: No. I am saying that a mind that’s willing to listen...

Jayakar: But, please listen to me, sir. Do you think a mind can be in that state of listening?

Krishnamurti: That’s very simple.

Jayakar: Is it?

Krishnamurti: Yes. I say just listen to a story that I am telling you. You are interested. Your mind is quiet; you are eager to see what the story is about and so on.

Jayakar: I’m sorry, sir, it doesn’t happen that way.

Krishnamurti: No?

Jayakar: No.

Krishnamurti: Just a minute. Don’t say no. I ask you, Pupul, to listen to what I am saying.

Jayakar: I listen.

Krishnamurti: No. Wait, wait. Listen. I am going to explain what I mean by listening.

Jayakar: Yes.

Krishnamurti: I mean by ‘listening’ not only the listening with the sensory ear, but the listening with the ear that has no movement. That is really listening.

Listening is not translating; listening is not comparing; listening is not trying to find out. Listening is something that is complete. Now, when you listen, completely, without any movement, to a man who comes along and says, ‘Don’t go through all this diligent process, because it is false, because it is superficial’, what takes place? If you hear the truth of his statement, what takes place? What actually takes place when you see something really true?

Now, this diligent process is time-consuming. Right? I have no time. My life is so short. I’ve got so many problems, and you are adding another; be diligent. And I say, ‘Please, I am withered; I am worn out by problems, and you have introduced another problem’. So the man says, ‘I know you have got many problems which are all interrelated. Forget your problems for the moment and listen to me. That’s all’.

Jayakar: No, you are talking of a mind which is already mature. Such a mind, while listening to a statement like this...

Krishnamurti: You see, Pupul, we have made our minds so immature that we are incapable of listening to anything.

Jayakar: But you see, Krishnaji, you start by making things impossible.

Krishnamurti: Of course. See the truth. See something impossible that you have found. It has a tremendous...

Jayakar: Can I find the energy which is needed?

Krishnamurti: Yes.

Jayakar: Can I find the energy to deal with an impossible thing?

Krishnamurti: That’s what it is. This diligent affair has been possible. I say it is something so trivial.

Jayakar: I ask you, what is the mind which can deal with an impossible statement like that? What is that mind?

Krishnamurti: It’s very simple. That which is utterly impossible is non-existent. We think everything is possible. I am getting it.

Jayakar: See what you are getting at, sir. You are saying that it is non-existent. So with a non-existent mind...

Krishnamurti: No, no. Look, Pupulji, just a minute. Can we both, you and I, agree - even temporarily - that this diligent process has really led nowhere? Can we see that this process has led to various activities - some of which may be beneficial - but that this process, this inquiry which says ‘I must go to the very source of things’ is not the way?

Jayakar: Yes, obviously. I would accept that.

Krishnamurti: That’s all. If you accept that it’s not through a diligent awareness...

Jayakar: But, sir, even to come to a point when I say that it is so...

Krishnamurti: What has happened to a mind that says that this is too trivial, too superficial? What is the quality of your mind?

Jayakar: I know what you are trying to say, sir.

Krishnamurti: No, you answer my question. What is the quality of a mind which has been caught in the process of diligent inquiry when it sees that the process which it has been caught in has no deep, fundamental value? This diligent process will not lead or help to comprehend or come upon or uncover the origin. This process is time-consuming. The other may have no time at all.

Jayakar: But, look at the danger of what you are saying. The danger is that I will not be concerned with sweeping the room.

Krishnamurti: No. I am inquiring into myself. That very inquiry demands that the mind and the heart - my whole existence - is orderly.

Jayakar: You start with the impossible.

Krishnamurti: (With great energy) Of course, I start with the impossible, Pupul, otherwise... Pupul, what is possible? You have done all that’s possible.

Jayakar: No, sir.

Krishnamurti: No, no. Man has done everything that’s possible. Man has fasted, sacrificed; man has done everything to find the origin of things. Man has done all that has been possible, and that has led nowhere. That’s what I am saying - possibility has led nowhere. It has led to certain benefits - social, and so on. It has also led to a great deal of misery for mankind. So, the man tells me that this diligent process is time-consuming and, therefore, time-binding. He tells me that as long as I am doing this, I am just scratching the surface. The surface may be the most extraordinary, pleasant and ennobling thing - but it’s just the surface. If you grant - no, not merely grant but actually see, actually feel - in your blood, as it were - that this is false, you will have already stepped out of something that is the ordinary into something that is extraordinary.

And we are not willing to do that. We want to go through all this. We treat it like learning a language. To learn a language, discipline, diligence, attention and so on are necessary. We carry the same mentality into the other. That’s what I object to.

Jayakar: I put aside the other.

Krishnamurti: Aha. It is not a game that we are playing.

Jayakar: No, I am not playing a game. One puts aside the other.

Krishnamurti: Which means, careful Pupul...

Jayakar: That even this seeing, this listening, is at an end - if I may put it this way.

Krishnamurti: Which means - what? That the movement of diligence has stopped - right? Of course. If that is false, it has gone. So what has happened to the mind - the mind that has been caught in diligent inquiry and so on, all of which is time-bound, and has been seen by me to be utterly superficial? What is the state of my mind? Right? It is a fresh mind. It is a totally new mind. And such a mind is necessary to inquire into, to uncover the origin.

If I talked like this to a very disciplined, ‘religious’ man, he wouldn’t even bother to listen. He would say, ‘All this is nonsense’. But you, in our dialogue, say, ‘Let’s go into it’ and, so, you put yourself into a position of listening. Find out.

Now, such a mind has no bondage to time. You see, the diligent process is to become something; it is to clarify, to understand, to go beyond. This mind has no beyond - it is not becoming something.

Could you, would you, go as far as that?

Jayakar: You see, the moment movement ends...

Krishnamurti: No, I ask you, would you go so far as to see the fact that such a mind cannot have any kind of dependence, any kind of attachment?

Jayakar: Yes, that I see. Sir, all that which you have talked about is the movement of becoming.

Krishnamurti: That’s right. All that is the perpetuation of the self in a different form, in a different network of words. You see, if you tell me this, I will want to find the source. And when I start out to uncover the source - which is, for me, a passion - I will want to find out, I will want to uncover the origin of all life. When there is that uncovering of origins, then my life, my actions - everything - is different.

That’s why I see that the understanding of the diligent process is a time-consuming fact which is destructive. It may be necessary in order to learn a technique, but this is not a technique to be learnt. (Long pause)

Jayakar: Sir, you have an antique mind - a mind of great antiquity.

Krishnamurti: What?

Jayakar: You have an antique mind - ‘antique’ in the sense that it contains the whole of human...

Krishnamurti: You see, Pupul, that’s why it’s important to understand that I am the world.

Jayakar: No one else would make that kind of statement but you.

Krishnamurti: And one must make it. Otherwise, when you see all the destruction, the brutality, the killing, the wars - every form of violence that has never stopped - where are you? A man who loved wouldn’t be British or Israeli or Arab. A man who loved couldn’t kill another. I see this process has been going on for thousands and thousands of years - everybody trying to become something. And all the diligent, religious workers are helping man to become something - to achieve illumination, to achieve enlightenment. It’s so absurd.

Jayakar: With you, sir, the whole movement of the dormant has ended.

Krishnamurti: That is, (Laughs) ‘diligence’ has ended. Becoming has ended. Pupul, let us not make this into some elitist understanding. Any person who pays attention, who wants to hear, who is passionate and not just casual about it and who, really, says, ‘I must find the source of life’, will listen. He will listen - not to me; he will just listen. It’s in the air.

Brockwood Park, 21 June 1982

12. Brain, Mind, Emptiness

PUPUL JAYAKAR: Sir, recently I read a short report in one of the newspapers that a spaceship had been released which would travel to the outer spaces of the universe. It would be part of the universe, and there would be no ending to it because there would be no friction, no time.

Krishnamurti: Yes.

Jayakar: My question is: Is there a within of the self, of the human brain, the human mind - call it what you will - is there a within of things, whether of man, of the tree, of nature, which is a space without ending, and is it a mirror image of that vastness which exists?

Krishnamurti: Are you asking, if I may repeat what you’ve said, whether there is or there can be a space without end, an eternity outside time within the human brain? I’d like to distinguish between the brain and the mind. You see, we can speculate a great deal - like philosophers have done - but that speculation is not actuality.

Jayakar: No, but if you do not posit a thing, then you cannot even...

Krishnamurti: No. I want to be clear on this point. Are we now, in our conversation, speculating and theorizing, or are we really trying to find out, in ourselves, whether there is something that is immense, whether there is, in actuality, a movement which is not of time but which is eternal?

Jayakar: How do you start inquiring? Do you start by examination or by posing the question? If you don’t pose the question...

Krishnamurti: We’ve posed the question.

Jayakar: Now, what comes out of it - speculation or examination - depends on how you approach it; but the question has to be posed.

Krishnamurti: We have put the question: Can the brain realize the truth as to whether there is eternity or not? That’s the question, right?

Jayakar: Yes.

Krishnamurti: Now, you ask: How do you begin to inquire into it? How do you begin to feel, gently, hesitantly, your way into this really fundamental question? Whether man is bound to time forever or whether the brain can realize itself - not imaginatively, not romantically, but actually - in a state of eternity, is a question that has been asked for thousands of years. And that is the question we’re asking too.

Jayakar: You started by drawing a distinction between the brain and the mind.

Krishnamurti: Yes.

Jayakar: Would you, please, elaborate?

Krishnamurti: First, we are saying that the brain or at least some part of it is conditioned. That conditioning is brought about through experience. That conditioning is knowledge and memory. And as experience, knowledge and memory are limited, thought is limited.

Jayakar: Yes.

Krishnamurti: Now, we have been functioning within the area of thought.

Jayakar: Yes.

Krishnamurti: And to discover something new, there has to be, at least temporarily, a period when thought is not in movement, when thought is in abeyance.

Jayakar: The brain is a material thing.

Krishnamurti: Yes.

Jayakar: It has its own activity.

Krishnamurti: Yes, it has its own activity which is not imposed by thought.

Jayakar: But for centuries the operation of the brain has been the operation of thought.

Krishnamurti: That’s all. That is what we are saying, namely that the whole movement of the brain - at least that part of the brain which has been used - is conditioned by thought, and thought is always limited and therefore it is conditioned to conflict. That which is limited must create division.

Jayakar: What is the mind then?

Krishnamurti: The mind is a wholly different dimension which has no contact with thought. Let me explain. The brain - that part of the brain which has been functioning as an instrument of thought - has been conditioned, and as long as that part of the brain remains in that state there is no communication - entire communication - with the mind. So, when that conditioning is not, then that mind which is totally on a different dimension, communicates with the brain and acts - using thought.

Jayakar: But you’ve already posited...

Krishnamurti: Oh, definitely.

Jayakar: A state which is outside the realm of thought.

Krishnamurti: That’s right - outside. And, therefore, outside the realm of...

Jayakar: Time.

Krishnamurti: Yes, time.

Jayakar: As time seems to be the essential core of this problem...

Krishnamurti: Time and thought.

Jayakar: Thought is a product of time. I mean, in a sense, thought is time.

Krishnamurti: That’s it, that’s the real point. Where do you start, you mean?

Jayakar: No, no. Perhaps if we could go into this whole business of the flow of time, and at what instant interception is possible...

Krishnamurti: What do you mean ‘interception’? I don’t quite understand the usage of that word. Who intercepts?

Jayakar: I’m not talking of an interceptor, but of the flow...

Krishnamurti: The ending of it.

Jayakar: I was going to use another word, but you could use the word ‘ending’.

Krishnamurti: Let’s use simpler words.

Jayakar: Time is from a past immemorial.

Krishnamurti: Yes - which is thought.

Jayakar: Thought is also from a past immemorial, projecting into a future, which is also eternal.

Krishnamurti: In terms of thought.

Jayakar: Eternal.

Krishnamurti: No. (Emphatic) The future is conditioned by the past - as a human psyche.

Jayakar: Yes. So, unless human civilization, human culture ends, unless the human being ceases to be...

Krishnamurti: Ceases to be conditioned, right?

Jayakar: Yes, but you will still use thought.

Krishnamurti: No.

Jayakar: The content will undergo a change, but the mechanism of thought will continue.

Krishnamurti: Now, let’s put it this way. Thought is the chief instrument we have. Right?

Jayakar: Yes.

Krishnamurti: Thousands of years of various efforts and actions have not only made that instrument dull, but that instrument has also reached the end of its tether. Thought-time is limited, conditioned, divided, and in a perpetual state of turmoil. Now, can that end? That’s the question.

Jayakar: Now, this movement of the past as thought, as the yesterday...

Krishnamurti: As today.

Jayakar: What is the ‘today’?

Krishnamurti: The ‘today’ is the movement of the past modified - memory. We are a bundle of memories.

Jayakar: That is true, but the contact with time...

Krishnamurti: What do you mean ‘contact with time’? Time is thought.

Jayakar: No, but the contact with time, as a psychological process, is in the present, isn’t it?

Krishnamurti: Pupul, let’s be very careful. Time is thought. Don’t separate time as if it were something different from thought. It is time-thought.

Jayakar: Yes. I’m using the past, present and future...

Krishnamurti: Are you asking: What is the ‘now’?

Jayakar: It’s ‘interception’ that I’m talking about. Let me use my word till I get it wiped away.

Krishnamurti: All right, all right; ‘interception’, but I don’t quite understand.

Jayakar: Interception is ‘contact with’ - contact with the fact.

Krishnamurti: Contact with the fact that the whole movement of thought...

Jayakar: Not even that. Just contact with ‘what is’.

Krishnamurti: Which is the ‘now’.

Jayakar: Which is whatever is. Your statement just now, and my listening to it, is the contact with ‘what is’.

Krishnamurti: May I put it in the way that I understand it? The past, the present and the future is a movement of time-thought. How do you realize it? How do you come to see the truth of it, the fact of it?

Jayakar: No, sir, there is such a thing as tactile touch.

Krishnamurti: Yes, touch. How do you touch this thing? How do you - to use your words - come into contact with this fact that I am a whole series of memories which is time-thought?

Jayakar: No, let us be more concrete. That I am going away this afternoon, and that I may be leaving you, is a thought.

Krishnamurti: It’s not a thought; it’s an actuality.

Jayakar: Actuality, yes; but out of that there is a certain pain of leaving you, in which the emotional, psychological elements come to cover up the fact.

Krishnamurti: Yes, which is - what?

Jayakar: So, what is to be contacted? Not the fact that I’m going away...

Krishnamurti: But what?

Jayakar: But this pain.

Krishnamurti: The pain? I understand. Are you asking: The pain of a thousand years and centuries - the pain of loneliness, sorrow, grief; the agony, the anxiety and all that - is that separate from the ‘me’ who feels it?

Jayakar: It may not be separate.

Krishnamurti: It is me. (Emphatic)

Jayakar: How do I touch it?

Krishnamurti: I don’t quite understand your usage of ‘How do I touch it?’

Jayakar: Sir, it’s only in the present...

Krishnamurti: I see what you mean.

Jayakar: It is only in the present that the whole of this edifice rests.

Krishnamurti: That’s what I said. The ‘now’ contains the past, the future and the present.

Jayakar: Yes.

Krishnamurti: Wait, let’s understand this. The present is the whole past and the future.

Jayakar: Yes.

Krishnamurti: This is the present. The present is me with all the memories of a thousand years, and those thousand years are being modified all the time. All that is the ‘now’ - the present.

Jayakar: But the present is also something which is not static. It’s over before...

Krishnamurti: Of course, of course. The moment you’ve said it, it’s gone.

Jayakar: It’s gone. So what is it that you actually see, what is it that you actually observe?

Krishnamurti: You actually observe the fact...

Jayakar: What fact?

Krishnamurti: The fact - just a minute - that the present is the whole movement of time and thought. You, actually, see the truth of that. You have - let’s not use the word ‘see’ - an insight, a perception, into the fact that the ‘now’ is all time and thought.

Jayakar: Does that perception emanate from the brain?

Krishnamurti: Either it comes by perceiving with the eyes and so on, or that perception is an insight which has nothing to do with time and thought.

Jayakar: But it arises within the brain?

Krishnamurti: Or does it arise outside the brain? That is your question, right?

Jayakar: Yes, it’s very important.

Krishnamurti: I know - that’s why I want to be clear. Is it within the sphere of the brain or is it that insight comes when there is freedom from conditioning, which is the operation of the mind? - That is supreme intelligence, you follow?

Jayakar: No, I don’t follow.

Krishnamurti: Let’s be clear. The brain is conditioned by time and thought, time-thought. As long as that conditioning remains, insight is not possible. You may have occasional insight into something, but not pure insight, which means the comprehension of the totality of things - I use the word ‘totality’ and not ‘wholeness’, because that word is now being used so much. That insight is not of time-thought, and it is the perception of completeness. Therefore that insight is part of that brain which is in a different dimension.

Jayakar: Without sight there cannot be insight.

Krishnamurti: That’s all I’m saying.

Jayakar: Sir, perceiving, listening - which is contained in perceiving - seems to me the essential essence of insight.

Krishnamurti: Would you repeat that again, please, slowly?

Jayakar: Let us take the word ‘insight’. It means ‘seeing into’.

Krishnamurti: Seeing into.

Jayakar: Seeing into seeing?

Krishnamurti: No, just a minute; let’s look at that word ‘seeing’. Insight into or the comprehension of the totality, of the vastness of something, is possible only when there is the cessation of thought and time. Thought and time are limited; therefore such limitation cannot have insight.

Jayakar: To understand what you are saying, I have to have an open ear and eyes that see. Out of that sound, out of that form, out of that whole...

Krishnamurti: Yes, out of the whole meaning of the words, and so on...

Jayakar: Arises a seeing which goes beyond. I’m trying to get at something.

Krishnamurti: What are you trying to get at? I don’t quite...

Jayakar: You talk of insight. Now, insight cannot arise without attention.

Krishnamurti: No, wait; don’t introduce the word ‘attention’.

Jayakar: Or sight, seeing.
